Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/medium/am0c20542_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c20542&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">The abundance of the available sodium sources has led to rapid progress in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), making them potential candidates for immediate replacement of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs). However, commercialization of SIBs has been hampered by their fading efficiency due to the sodium consumed in the formation of solid–electrolyte interphase (SEI) when using hard carbon (HC) anodes. Herein, Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sodium salt is introduced as a highly efficient, cost-effective, and safe cathode sodiation additive. This sustainable sodium salt has an oxidation potential of ∼4.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na°, so it could be practically implemented into SIBs. Moreover, for the first time, we have also revealed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) that in addition to the compensating Na<sup>+</sup> ions spent in the SEI layer, the high specific capacity and capacity retention observed from electrochemical measurements are due to the formation of a thinner and more stable cathode–electrolyte interphase (CEI) on the P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> while using such a cathode sodiation additive. Half-cell studies with P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes show a 27% increase in the specific capacity (164 mAh g<sub>P2</sub><sup>–1</sup>) with cathode sodiation additives. Full-cell studies with the HC anode show a 4 times increase in the specific capacity of P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. This work provides notable insights into and avenues toward the development of SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D11814%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DAntonio%2BJ.%2BFern%25C3%25A1ndez-Ropero%252C%2BMaider%2BZarrabeitia%252C%2BGiorgio%2BBaraldi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D10%26contentID%3Dacsami.0c20542%26title%3DImproved%2BSodiation%2BAdditive%2Band%2BIts%2BNuances%2Bin%2Bthe%2BPerformance%2BEnhancement%2Bof%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D11821%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292200&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Layers"> Layers </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291301&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Salts"> Salts </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium"> Sodium </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292316&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transition metals"> Transition metals </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sacrificial+Sodium+Salt&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sacrificial%20sodium%20salt">sacrificial sodium salt</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode%E2%88%92electrolyte+Interphase&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode%E2%88%92electrolyte%20interphase">cathode−electrolyte interphase</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodiation+Cathode+Additive&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodiation%20cathode%20additive">sodiation cathode additive</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Post-mortem+Analysis&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_post-mortem%20analysis">post-mortem analysis</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are the current immediate alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) owing to the abundance of sodium and the practicality of the technology. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Most lithium battery systems like Li/S and Li-metal are still in the lab scale, with very few making up to the commercial level, the notable exception being the Li°||LiFePO<sub>4</sub> system from Blue Solutions using a polymer electrolyte. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> Sodium does not intercalate appreciably in graphite (≈NaC<sub>64</sub>), and the development of the hard carbon (HC) technology has been a turning point in enabling rapid improvement in SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 to 5">(3−5)</a> One of the major limiting factors that the current SIBs face is the huge irreversibility observed in the first cycle arising from the HC anode electrodes (nearly 40%). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> This irreversibility accounts for the Na<sup>+</sup> ions to be spent from the cathode in building up the solid–electrolyte interphase (SEI). Moreover, some sodium deficient cathodes (Na<sub>0.66</sub>TO<sub>2</sub>; T = transition metal/s) with stable structures are not able to supply enough Na<sup>+</sup> ions, resulting in poor electrochemical performance. Foreseeing this drawback, our group has previously reported the concept and use of sacrificial sodium salts as a cathode sodiation additive for SIBs to overcome this drawback. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> Though the concept has been proven, the sacrificial salts identified have their own disadvantages in terms of toxicity, cost-effectiveness, ease of synthesis, and working efficiency that still need to be improved. For example, NaN<sub>3</sub> works well with the P2 phase cathode materials, but its explosive nature remains a concern. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7,8)</a> on the other hand, seems to be environmentally friendly, efficient, and safe to be employed in SIBs, but most of the drawbacks associated with it are related to the cost of the raw material, i.e., squaric acid, which is still a major cost-increasing factor. Some of the sacrificial sodium salts reported by other groups, such as Na<sub>3</sub>P, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9−11)</a> also have their own disadvantages. Na<sub>3</sub>P is known for its toxicity (phosphine) and flammable nature, with a requirement of handling an inert atmosphere, incompatible with the current technology. Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> liberates oxygen, a part of which is O<sub>2</sub>* upon oxidation, that could react with the electrolyte and the sodiated HC; Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, on the other hand, has in practice a high oxidation potential ∼4.4 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na°. This made us to explore other sodium salts that could lead us to an optimum cathode sodiaiton additive  covering all aspects, namely, safety, cost-effectiveness, ease of synthesis, environmental friendliness, feasibility of utilization with an optimum oxidation potential (∼4.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na°), and scalability. Herein, we introduce a Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sacrificial sodium salt that is available commercially as Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O in effectively overcoming the drawbacks currently limiting SIBs. The effect and role of such a cathode sodiation additive on the variation in composition and structure of the cathode–electrolyte interphase (CEI) is still unclear. We have also attempted, for the first time, to study in-depth the effect of these cathode sodiation additive on the formation of a CEI at the cathode surface and understand the origin of improvement, with a P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode material.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Sacrificial Sodium Salt Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Sodium mesoxalate monohydrate (Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O), which is the gem diol (NaO<sub>2</sub>CC(OH)<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>2</sub>Na), was purchased from Sigma-Aldrich. The sodium salt was heat-treated at 200 °C under vacuum for 12 h to remove the water molecule, and finally, the sacrificial sodium salt with a yellowish tinge (Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub>) was obtained. The sodium salt was further ground using a mortar and pestle and dried overnight at 50 °C under vacuum prior to use. A composite with sacrificial sodium salt and KJ600 (70:30 wt % ratio) was prepared and used as an cathode sodiation additive in the P2 cathode electrode. KJ600 was selected over C65 to overcome the salt’s insulating nature and derive maximum capacity, which requires carbon with a higher specific surface (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/suppl_file/am0c20542_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> in the Supporting Information).</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Cathode Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode was synthesized using our previously reported technique. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> Stoichiometric amounts of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, and TiO<sub>2</sub> powders were mixed in a mortar. The resultant powder was pelletized (pellets of diameter 20 mm and thickness 3 mm) and annealed at 1000 °C for 6 h under ambient atmosphere, and the pellets, once cooled, were quickly transferred to a glovebox to avoid moisture absorption.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Anode Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Hard carbon anodes derived from peanut shells were used in this study. The synthesis technique is given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/suppl_file/am0c20542_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information S2</a>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a></div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was carried out under inert atmosphere by heating from room temperature up to 500 °C, at a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> on a NETZSCH simultaneous thermal analyzer (STA) 449 F3 Jupiter. Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy was performed using a Vertex 70 (Bruker) FTIR spectrometer with ATR setup. X-ray diffraction measurements were carried out at room temperature using a BRUKER-D8 ADVANCE X-ray diffractometer at 2θ values of 10–80° and a scan rate of 2° min<sup>–1</sup>. Post-mortem analysis was carried out using a Quanta 200 FEG scanning electron microscope. All electrochemical measurements were carried out on a VMP3 (Biologic, Claix, France) potentiostat.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.5.  Electrode Preparation/Cell Configuration</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The cathode active material, P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, was mixed with Carbon SP (Csp-Timcal) and poly(vinylidene difluoride) (PVDF) binder (Solvay) in the ratio of 80:10:10 using <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) as the solvent. The slurries were cast on battery-grade aluminum foil using the doctor blade technique and dried at room temperature. Further drying was carried out at 120 °C under vacuum for 12 h. For electrodes containing cathode sodiation additive, 34.2 g of composite (SS/KJ600 of 70:30 wt %) was added to 100 mg of the mix P2/C65/PVDF (80:10:10 wt %). Therefore, the sacrificial sodium salt in the electrode was 17.83%. This was added to approximately have a ratio of P2/salt composite to be 70:30 wt %. Sodium metal was used as counter and reference electrodes for half-cells. The electrolyte consisted of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC) (EC/PC = 1:1 wt % ratio), in which 2 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) was included as the electrolyte additive, and glass fiber as the separator. CR2032 coin-cells were assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox (UniLab, M-BRAUN, O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 1 ppm). Post-mortem analysis of the cells was carried out by rinsing the cycled electrodes with dimethyl carbonate (DMC) and drying them under argon to ensure the removal of any salt deposits from the electrolyte on the electrodes. Full-cells of P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were tested using HC electrodes as anodes consisting of 80% HC, 10% carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) binder (Sigma-Aldrich), and 10% Csp cast on battery-grade aluminum foil. C-rates are calculated on the basis of the full theoretical specific capacity of P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (245.19 mAh·g<sup>–1</sup> for 0.67 Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation/intercalation).</div></div><div id="sec2_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">2.6.  Cathode–Electrolyte Interphase Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed to characterize the cathode–electrolyte interphase of P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> electrodes mixed with Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sacrificial sodium salt. The XPS experiments were carried out on electrodes kept at open-circuit voltage (OCV), as well as, on cycled electrodes recovered after the 1<sup>st</sup> and 10<sup>th</sup> cycles (electrodes were cycled at 0.1C). The collected electrodes, after disassembling the cells, were rinsed with DMC and transferred to an XPS vacuum chamber using an Ar-filled transfer holder. A non-monochromatic Mg Kα (<i>h</i>ν = 1253.6 eV) X-ray source and a Phoibos 150 XPS spectrometer equipped with a microchannel plate and delay line detector (DLD) were used. The scans of each element were acquired in a fixed-analyzer transmission mode with an X-ray source power of 100 W, pass energy of 20 eV, and energy step of 0.1 eV. The photoelectron spectra were calibrated using the graphitic peak (sp<sup>2</sup>,−C═C−) at 284.4 eV, corresponding to the conductive additive (Csp) of the electrodes. The data fitting was carried out with CasaXPS software, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> using a nonlinear Shirley-type background and 70% Gaussian and 30% Lorentzian profile function.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">First, the synthesized P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> was analyzed by XRD and Rietveld refinement fits to a hexagonal <i><i>P</i></i>6<sub>3</sub><i>/<i>mmc</i></i> symmetry without any notable impurity peaks as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/suppl_file/am0c20542_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. The morphology of the particles shows large size distribution as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/suppl_file/am0c20542_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. Sodium mesoxalate monohydrate (Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O) was subjected to thermogravimetric analysis to examine the pre-heating temperature required to remove the chemically bound water. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows that the pristine-hydrated sodium mesoxalic acid undergoes a weight loss at around 200 °C, indicating the removal of water. Further confirmations were made from FTIR analysis, where the pristine sample (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b) shows −OH stretching vibrational bands between 2500 and 3500 cm<sup>–1</sup> at room temperature.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/medium/am0c20542_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Thermogravimetric curve of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O sacrificial sodium salt and (b) FTIR spectra of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O sacrificial sodium salt after heating overnight at different temperatures under inert atmospheres.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c20542&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">On heating the samples at 150 °C for 12 h, the −OH stretching vibrational bands, although with a decrease in intensity, show incomplete removal of hydrated water. On increasing the temperature further up to 200 °C, the water of crystallization was completely removed with an absence of peaks at 2500 and 3500 cm<sup>–1</sup> in the FTIR spectra. X-ray diffraction peaks of the samples after the heat treatment show a decrease in the crystallinity of the samples, indicating removal of the water molecule, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/suppl_file/am0c20542_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. Therefore, the sodium mesoxalate monohydrate samples were pre-heated to 200 °C for 12 h under vacuum to obtain the pure sacrificial sodium salt, Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Compared to previous sacrificial sodium salts that we have reported for sodiation in SIBs (NaN<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub>), Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> seems to be the most cost-effective and safe in terms of raw material and synthesis procedure. Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> commonly known as sodium mesoxalate or sodium ketomalonate belongs to the family of dicarboxylic acids. The calculated theoretical capacity is around 331 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which makes it an efficient cathode sodiation additive  in SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">First, Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> was mixed with carbon (KJ600) (70:30 wt % ratio). The morphology of the sacrificial sodium salt mixed with carbon forming agglomerates is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/suppl_file/am0c20542_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. The sacrificial sodium salt/carbon composite was cycled with sodium metal anode up to 4.2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na° to determine the practical capacity that could be derived from the salt. The salt/carbon composite delivered a capacity of 310 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at ∼4.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na° (around 1.5 Na<sup>+</sup> ions), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a at 0.1C C-rate. This is equal to 94% of the theoretical capacity of the Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub>  (331 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for 2 Na<sup>+</sup> ions). The reaction mechanism for the Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sacrificial sodium salt is as shown below<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/medium/am0c20542_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span>The salt/composite was mixed as an cathode sodiation additive in P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes such that the sacrificial sodium salt constitutes only 17.83% of the cathode electrode composition. Our earlier studies showed the absence of any phase transition of the P2 phase even at 4.3 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na°; <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> therefore, a first formation step with an oxidation potential limit of up to 4.2 V was performed to ensure complete oxidation of the salt with consecutive cycles charged up to 4.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na°. The half-cells cycled with and without the sacrificial sodium salt are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b,c, respectively. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c, there is an extra capacity of 130 mAh g<sub>P2</sub><sup>–1</sup> in the 1<sup>st</sup> oxidation cycled contributed by the sacrificial sodium salt. The observed extra capacity disappears for the consecutive cycles, indicating a predominant oxidation of the sacrificial sodium salt mainly in the 1<sup>st</sup> cycle. Post-mortem analysis of cells after the 1<sup>st</sup> oxidation cycles with and without the sacrificial sodium salt is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/suppl_file/am0c20542_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>, confirming the formation of pores on the electrodes. This in turn has an additional benefit of better electrolyte wetting of the P2 phase cathode.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/medium/am0c20542_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Galvanostatic oxidation profile of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> + KJ600 (70:30 wt %) sacrificial sodium salt at a C-rate of 0.1C; (b) charge–discharge profiles of pristine P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>; and (c) pristine P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> + sacrificial sodium salt Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrodes in half-cells at 0.05C up to 4.2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na° in the first cycle and up to 4.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na° for the next five cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c20542&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The C-rate capability tests (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>), taken after the first formation step cycled at 0.05C shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>, indicate the superior performance, in terms of specific capacity, with Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sacrificial sodium salt at all applied C-rates.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/medium/am0c20542_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. C-rate capability and charge/discharge cycles of pristine P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (blue) and pristine P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> + sacrificial sodium salt Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (green) electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c20542&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Pristine + sacrificial sodium salt electrode delivers a specific capacity of 26 mAh g<sub>P2</sub><sup>–1</sup> at 10C, while negligible capacity was observed for cells without the sacrificial sodium salt. A 27% (35 mAh g<sub>P2</sub><sup>–1</sup>) increase in the charge/discharge capacity was achieved for cells cycled at 0.1C following the C-rate test with coulombic efficiency nearing 100% and a specific capacity of 164 mAh g<sub>P2</sub><sup>–1</sup> even after 140 cycles with the sacrificial sodium salt-incorporated electrodes. The carbon generated during salt decomposition could slightly improve the electrochemical performance of the electrode. However, the amount of carbon is too low to be the sole reason for such an enhancement. The main plausible reason should be a different configuration/chemistry of the CEI. This effectively means that in addition to the supply of additional Na<sup>+</sup> ions, a favorable interphase layer is formed, enabling better Na<sup>+</sup> ion transfer kinetics that contributes to two factors, namely, C-rate capability and capacity retention of the cells.</div><div class="NLM_p">HC with a bio-waste source from peanut shells was utilized as anode materials for full-cell configuration with P2 cathodes (with and without sacrificial sodium salt). First, half-cells of HC using sodium metal anode were cycled to estimate the capacity deliverance of the peanut shell-derived HCs. As seen from Supporting Information 2 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/suppl_file/am0c20542_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>), the first charge irreversibility was around 35% with a specific charge/discharge capacity of 200 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles. Full-cells cycled at 0.1C with the peanut shell-derived HC anodes and P2 cathodes with and without the sacrificial sodium salt are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. The full-cell with the sacrificial salt shows a remarkable performance with a capacity of 100 mAh g<sub>(P2)</sub><sup>–1</sup>, i.e., 4 times the capacity of the cell without the sacrificial sodium salts. These results suggest the use of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> as a versatile cathode sodiation additive for improvement of SIB performance. The addition of the sacrificial sodium salt implies a decrease in the total capacity of the electrode (60 mAh g<sup>-1</sup> of electrode material), but still continues to be higher than the capacity obtained for the salt-free electrode. Furthermore, the sacrificial sodium salts completely avoid the use of expensive additional cathode material to compensate the irreversibility of the HC anodes in a full-cell configuration. The factors that could contribute to the improvement of the specific capacity, rate capability, and capacity retention in both half- and full-cell configurations, i.e., using sodium metal and HC anode raised the curiosity to monitor the interfacial layer, namely, cathode–electrolyte interphase (CEI) that is ideally formed during the first cyles. Therefore, we embarked on the study of CEI layer of the P2 cathode with and without sacrificial sodium salts. Initially, post-mortem analyses of the cells after 10 cycles were carried out by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,e shows different surface morphologies of the P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> electrodes with and without sacrificial sodium salts. The surface morphology of the pristine P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> electrode was found to have cracks formed along the surface, whereas the electrodes with sacrificial sodium salts were found to be more homogeneous and the particles are well connected.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/medium/am0c20542_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Full-cells of P2 cathodes (a) with Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and (b) without Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sacrificial sodium salt additive vs HC anodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c20542&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/medium/am0c20542_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Surface morphology and (b–d) cross-sectional analysis of pristine electrodes after 10 cycles; (e) surface morphology and (f–h) cross-sectional analysis of electrodes with sacrificial sodium salts after 10 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c20542&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Cross-sectional analysis revealed two different morphologies of the CEI layer formed on the cathode surface. The pristine electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b–d) was covered with a thick sheet of CEI wrapping the P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> particles, while on the contrary, the CEI formed on the surface of the electrode with sacrificial sodium salt (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f–h) seemed to be more granular and homogeneous covering the P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode, as shown in the micrographs. Similar conclusions were made, with our earlier work on the P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode alone showing that a thicker CEI is formed. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> The granular and homogeneous nature of the CEI observed for the first time with a P2 cathode with sacrificial sodium salt raised further interest to know more about the CEI composition, leading us to XPS studies on the sacrificial sodium salt-incorporated P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes.</div><div class="NLM_p">The CEI formed on the surface of P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrodes was studied by XPS under the same experimental conditions carried out for P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> only containing electrodes in our previous work (for more details, see ref  <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> shows the evolution of the CEI layer, where the C 1s, F 1s, and O 1s photoelectron spectra are illustrated. The C1s photoelectron spectra show peaks at 284.4 eV, indicating a dominant contribution from the conductive carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> This suggests that the CEI formed on the P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> composite electrode is thinner in the presence of the sacrificial sodium salt, at least on the surface of the carbon. Our previous work has shown that the P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> electrodes were covered by a thicker CEI layer even after 10 cycles, since the peaks corresponding to conductive carbon were not clearly observed, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/suppl_file/am0c20542_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8.</a> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> Indeed, the intensity of the peaks corresponding to the PVDF binder, which is one of the other electrode components, at 286.4 and 290.9 eV (C 1s, assigned to −CH<sub>2</sub>– and −CF<sub>2</sub> groups, respectively) and 688.0 eV (F 1s assigned to −CF<sub>2</sub>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> decreases after the first cycles, suggesting that the CEI layer is formed on the surface of the binder. The formation of the CEI layer also occurs on the surface of the P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> that could be confirmed by the decrease in the intensity of the M–O signal (529.5 eV) in O 1s photoelectron spectra, which correspond to Mn/Fe/Ti–O bonding. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/medium/am0c20542_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. C 1s, F 1s, and O 1s photoelectron spectra of P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode mixed with Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sacrificial sodium salt electrode at different states of charge during electrochemical cycling.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c20542&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In the present work, the observed higher M–O intensity of the peak (ratio between M–O and −CO<sub>3</sub> peak) from P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> after the 1<sup>st</sup> cycle compared with our previous work on P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>/Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/suppl_file/am0c20542_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>, suggests that a much thinner layer is formed in the presence of sacrificial sodium salt, which is in agreement with the C 1s region. This thinner CEI formation could be a consequence of the salt decomposition, during which CO<sub>2</sub> gas is released that can “break” and/or create cracks simultaneously during the formation of CEI layer in the 1<sup>st</sup> cycle. On the other hand, the alkoxides formed on the negative electrode diffuse through the electrolyte to react with the CO<sub>2</sub> formed at the positive electrode that fills the cracks in the CEI. Moreover, the reaction products not only fill the cracks/the porous structure but also inhibit further build-up of CEI, thus controlling its thickness. At this point, it is worth mentioning that the gas generated during the oxidation of the sacrificial sodium salt is not a major concern during the cell fabrication. Normal practices in industries involve sealing of the cells after the first cycle to release ethylene and build-up of hydrogen gases; the same procedure could be adopted for the cells with sacrificial salts/sodiation additives. Although the CEI layer is thinner when sacrificial sodium salt is employed, the composition of the layer is the same as P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. The CEI layer is composed of carbonaceous and oxygenated species observed in C 1s and O 1s photoelectron spectra, which are formed due to the decomposition reactions of the carbonate-based solvents from the electrolyte with the formation of species like hydrocarbons (−CH−) at 285.3 eV (C 1s) and species with carbon bound to one oxygen (−COC−) at 286.1 eV (C 1s) and 533 eV (O 1s), two oxygens (−CO<sub>2</sub>−) at 287–289 eV (C 1s) and 532.9 and 534.2 eV (O 1s), and three oxygens (−CO<sub>3</sub>) at 290.7 eV (C 1s) and 532.1 eV (O 1s). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 16, 19 and 20">(16,19,20)</a> In other words, the CEI layer is composed of PEO oligomers, alkyl carbonate, and sodium carbonate. Among fluorinated species, the F 1s photoelectron spectra show that at low binding energies (685 eV), a peak corresponding to NaF can be found, while at higher binding energies (687 eV), a peak corresponding to NaPF<sub>6</sub>, Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>PF<i><sub>z</sub></i>, and/or Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>PO<i><sub>y</sub></i>F<i><sub>z</sub></i> is observed. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> The latter species are as a result of the NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt decomposition reactions, while the presence of NaF is due to the PVdF dehydrofluorination from the basic alkoxides diffusing from the negative electrode, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt decomposition (NaPF<sub>6</sub> ⇔ NaF + PF<sub>5</sub>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> and/or FEC decomposition. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> The mentioned carbonaceous/oxygenated and NaF species are formed at the initial stage (OCV), while the phosphates and fluorophosphates are formed at higher voltages (note that the peak at 688 eV at OCV shifted at lower binding energies due to the presence of NaPF<sub>6</sub> decomposition products). Finally, the C 1s spectrum after 10 cycles shows extra peaks at high binding energies (BE, &gt;292 eV), which might be due to the presence of fluorinated species (due to the high BE) other than the contribution from PVDF, as shown in the F 1s spectrum. Regarding the stability of the CEI layer during cycling, it can be concluded that the CEI layer is more stable when P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is mixed with the sacrificial sodium salt, which is in agreement with the electrochemical performance. However, alkyl carbonates [NaO–(CO)O–CH<sub>2</sub>–R] are further decomposed upon electrochemical cycling. This might be due to the carbonate-based solvents of the electrolytes, such as EC, PC, and FEC that are metastable and undergo continuous decomposition in the consecutive cycles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> To summarize, the analysis from XPS data provides information that the CEI layer composition of P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> + Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sacrificial sodium salt is the same as previously studied for P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. The cathode–electrolyte interphase is formed by hydrocarbons and ether species such as PEO oligomers, alkyl carbonate, sodium carbonates, fluorophosphates, and NaF.</div><div class="NLM_p">The main differences between the P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and the P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> + Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>, are that the CEI formed during the OCV is smoothened and its growth controlled due to gas evolution during oxidation. This helps in building up a thinner and stable CEI leading to better capacity retention and specific capacity in addition to the advantage of compensating the loss of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in building up the SEI at the HC anode. In the case of P2 phase electrodes without the sacrificial sodium salt, the CEI grows thicker after the 1<sup>st</sup> charge, thus leading to poor capacity retention for consecutive cycles as well as loss of specific capacity.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/medium/am0c20542_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Schematic of the evolution of CEI layer for P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with sacrificial sodium salt (top) and P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (bottom).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/asset/images/large/am0c20542_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c20542&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">A safe, cost-effective, and highly efficient Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sodium salt has been shown as the most suitable candidate for cathode sodiation additive in SIBs. The sodium salt proposed herein is also easy to synthesize, thus finding it quite useful for small-scale as well as large-scale applications. A first-time study of the CEI layer with sacrificial sodium salt incorporated, by SEM and XPS analysis, reveals that this sacrificial sodium salt leads to better surface morphology of the electrodes. In addition, the sacrificial sodium salt plays a major role in the formation of a thin and stable CEI layer, without any chemical compositional changes of the CEI, compared to the pristine P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, thereby improving the overall cell performance. Full-cell studies show a 4 times improvement considering the total mass of the electrode, thereby proving it to be an ad hoc source of sodiation for SIBs. This sodium salt paves a new way in the field of cathode sodiation additive, leading to a rapid improvement of sodium batteries.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-5"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c20542?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c20542</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Comparison of charge profiles of sacrificial sodium salt/carbon mixture with C65 and KJ600 carbon (Figure S1); synthesis procedure and galvanostatic cycling and coulombic efficiency of HC in half-cell configuration with sodium metal anode (Figure S2); Rietveld refinement of pristine P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> powder XRD pattern (Figure S3); SEM micrographs of pristine P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> at different magnifications (Figure S4); X-ray diffractogram of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> after different heat treatments (Figure S5); SEM micrographs of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> salt after composite mixing with carbon (Figure S6); SEM micrographs of P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> mixed with Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub> before cycling and after the first cycle (Figure S7); comparison of the C 1s photoelectron spectra of P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> from ref  <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> and P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> + Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub>—this work—after 10 cycles (Figure S8); and comparison of the O 1s photoelectron spectra of P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> from ref  <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> and P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> + Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub>—this work (Figure S9) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/suppl_file/am0c20542_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c20542/suppl_file/am0c20542_si_001.pdf">am0c20542_si_001.pdf (727.88 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.0c20542" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

Hard C (HC) is the state-of-the-art anode material for Na-ion batteries due to its excellent overall performance, wide availability, and relatively low cost. However, disagreements remain in the field, particularly on the fundamental questions of ion transfer and storage and the ideal HC structure for high performance.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard carbons for sodium-ion batteries: Structure, analysis, sustainability, and electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dou, Xinwei; Hasa, Ivana; Saurel, Damien; Vaalma, Christoph; Wu, Liming; Buchholz, Daniel; Bresser, Dominic; Komaba, Shinichi; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Today (Oxford, United Kingdom)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Hard carbons are extensively studied for application as anode materials in sodium-ion batteries, but only recently a great interest has been focused toward the understanding of the sodium storage mechanism and the comprehension of the structure-function correlation.  Although several interesting mechanisms have been proposed, a general mechanism explaining the obsd. electrochem. processes is still missing, which is essentially originating from the remaining uncertainty on the complex hard carbons structure.  The achievement of an in-depth understanding of the processes occurring upon sodiation, however, is of great importance for a rational design of optimized anode materials.In this review, we aim at providing a comprehensive overview of the up-to-date known structural models of hard carbons and their correlation with the proposed models for the sodium-ion storage mechanisms.  In this regard, a particular focus is set on the most powerful anal. tools to study the structure of hard carbons (upon de-/sodiation) and a crit. discussion on how to interpret and perform such anal.  Targeting the eventual commercialization of hard carbon anodes for sodium-ion batteries - after having established a fundamental understanding - we close this review with a careful evaluation of potential strategies to ensure a high degree of sustainability, since this is undoubtedly a crucial parameter to take into account for the future large-scale prodn. of hard carbons.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2013.10.008"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Singh, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Acebedo, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Casas-Cabanas, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shanmukaraj, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Armand, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">An Approach to Overcome First Cycle Irreversible Capacity in P<sub>2</sub>-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub></span>. <i>Electrochem.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An approach to overcome first cycle irreversible capacity in P2-Na2/3[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Singh, Gurpreet; Acebedo, Begona; Cabanas, Montse Casas; Shanmukaraj, Devaraj; Armand, Michel; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemistry Communications</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The Earth-abundant high capacity Na2/3[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2 sodium-ion battery cathode material has been synthesized by conventional solid state synthesis.  X-ray diffraction measurement shows the formation of the single phase Na2/3[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2.  SEM shows particle size in the micron range having hexagonal morphol. with high aspect ratio.  To circumvent the first cycle irreversible capacity loss in Na2/3[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2, NaN3 has been used as a source of extra Na ions added to the cathode mix.  While pristine P2 type Na2/3[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2 exhibits an irreversible capacity of 59 mAh/g, it reduces to 27 mAh/g after adding 5 wt.% NaN3 with concomitant nitrogen evolution in the half cell configuration. Sodium-ion batteries have gained much attention for their potential application in large-scale stationary energy storage due to the low cost and abundant sodium sources in the earth.  However, the electrochem. performance of sodium-ion full cells (SIFCs) suffers severely from the irreversible consumption of sodium ions of cathode during the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation of hard carbon anode.  Here, a high-efficiency cathode sodiation compensation reagent, sodium oxalate (Na2C2O4), which possesses both a high theor. capacity of 400 mA h g-1 and a capacity utilization as high as 99%, is proposed. The IL-based system reveals better electrochem. performance at room temp. than the carbonate electrolyte-based one at 0.1C and 1C, esp. in terms of cycling stability, with a 97% capacity retention after 100 deep cycles (0.1C).  The electrode/electrolyte interface is thoroughly studied in both systems by means of XPS and SEM so as proof that the formed interface is crucial to optimizing the electrochem. performance of NIBs.  The carbonate-based system shows a thin, inhomogeneous, and unstable interface layer, while the IL-based one exhibits an even thinner but homogeneous and more stable interface, which may result in safer and longer-lasting NIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.201700628"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hasa, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hekmatfar, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Diemant, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jürgen

                        cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Pectin, Hemicellulose, or Lignin? Impact of the Biowaste Source on the Performance of Hard Carbons for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dou, Xinwei; Hasa, Ivana; Hekmatfar, Maral; Diemant, Thomas; Behm, R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons are currently the most widely used neg. electrode materials in Na-ion batteries.  This is due to their promising electrochem. performance with capacities of 200-300 mAh/g and stable long-term cycling.  However, an abundant and cheap C source is necessary in order to comply with the low-cost philosophy of Na-ion technol.  Many biol. or waste materials have been used to synthesize hard carbons but the impact of the precursors on the final properties of the anode material is not fully understood.  Here, the impact of the biomass source on the structural and electrochem. properties of hard carbons is unraveled by using different, representative types of biomass as examples.  The systematic structural and electrochem. investigation of hard carbons derived from different sources-namely corncobs, peanut shells, and waste apples, which are representative of hemicellulose-, lignin- and pectin-rich biomass, resp.-enables understanding and interlinking of the structural and electrochem. properties.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Waltron, J.</span>; <span class="NLM_string-name">Wincott, P.</span>; <span class="NLM_string-name">Fairley, N.</span>; <span class="NLM_string-name">Carrick, A.</span></span> <i>Peak Fitting Casa XPS: A Casa Pocket Book</i>; <span class="NLM_publisher-name">A.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Performance P2-Phase Na2/3Mn0.8Fe0.1Ti0.1O2 Cathode Material for Ambient-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Man Huon; Gonzalo, Elena; Sharma, Neeraj; Lopez del Amo, Juan Miguel; Armand, Michel; Avdeev, Maxim; Saiz Garitaonandia, Jose Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-performance Mn-rich P2-phase Na2/3Mn0.8Fe0.1Ti0.1O2 is synthesized by a ceramic method, and its stable electrochem. performance is demonstrated. 23Na solid-state NMR confirms the substitution of Ti4+ ions in the transition metal oxide layer and very fast Na+ mobility in the interlayer space.  The pristine electrode delivers a second charge/discharge capacity of 146.57/144.16 mA·h·g-1 and retains 95.09% of discharge capacity at the 50th cycle within the voltage range 4.0-2.0 V at C/10.  At 1C, the reversible specific capacity still reaches 99.40 mA·h·g-1, and capacity retention of 87.70% is achieved from second to 300th cycle.  In addn., the moisture-exposed electrode reaches reversible capacities of more than 130 and 80 mA·h·g-1 for C/10 and 1C, resp., with excellent capacity retention.  The correlation between overall electrochem. performance of both electrodes and crystal structural characteristics are investigated by neutron powder diffraction.  The stability of pristine electrode's crystallog. structure during the charge/discharge process has been investigated by in situ X-ray diffraction, where only a solid soln. reaction occurs within the given voltage range except for a small biphasic mechanism occurring at or below 2.2 V during the discharge process.  The relatively small substitution (20%) at the transition metal site leads to stable electrochem. performance, which is in part derived from the structural stability during electrochem. cycling.  Therefore, the small cosubstitution (e.g., with Ti and Fe) route suggests a possible new scope for the design of sodium-ion battery electrodes that are suitable for long-term cycling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1016/S0169-4332(00)00525-0"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Blyth, R.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">XPS studies of graphite electrode materials for lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Blyth, R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We compare graphite powders and carbon fibers as model materials for x-ray photoemission spectroscopy (XPS) studies of the effects of surface pretreatments.  For carbon fibers, the surface carbon percentage was found to vary from 70-95% depending on the surface treatment, with corresponding changes in the relative proportion of graphitic compared to C-O bonds, as detd. from C 1s curve fits.  In contrast, results from the graphite powders show very little change in surface chem. compn. and an essentially const. C 1s lineshape dominated by graphitic carbon.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Passivation Layer and Cathodic Redox Reactions in Sodium-Ion Batteries Probed by HAXPES</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Doubaji, Siham; Philippe, Bertrand; Saadoune, Ismael; Gorgoi, Mihaela; Gustafsson, Torbjorn; Solhy, Abderrahim; Valvo, Mario; Rensmo, Hakan; Edstroem, Kristina</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemSusChem</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The cathode material P2-NaxCo2/3Mn2/9Ni1/9O2, which could be used in Na-ion batteries, was investigated through synchrotron-based hard XPS (HAXPES).  Nondestructive anal. was made through the electrode/electrolyte interface of the first electrochem. cycle to ensure access to information not only on the active material, but also on the passivation layer formed at the electrode surface and referred to as the solid permeable interface (SPI).  This investigation clearly shows the role of the SPI and the complexity of the redox reactions.  Cobalt, nickel, and manganese are all electrochem. active upon cycling between 4.5 and 2.0 V; all are in the 4+ state at the end of charging.  Redn. to Co3+, Ni3+, and Mn3+ occurs upon discharging and, at low potential, there is partial reversible redn. to Co2+ and Ni2+.  A thin layer of Na2CO3 and NaF covers the pristine electrode and reversible dissoln./reformation of these compds. is obsd. during the first cycle.  The salt degrdn. products in the SPI show a dependence on potential.  Phosphates mainly form at the end of the charging cycle (4.5 V), whereas fluorophosphates are produced at the end of discharging (2.0 V).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b01375"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Muñoz-Márquez, M.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Composition and Evolution of the Solid-Electrolyte Interphase in Na2Ti3O7 Electrodes for Na-Ion Batteries: XPS and Auger Parameter Analysis</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Munoz-Marquez, Miguel A.; Zarrabeitia, Maider; Castillo-Martinez, Elizabeth; Eguia-Barrio, Aitor; Rojo, Teofilo; Casas-Cabanas, Montse</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na2Ti3O7 is considered a promising neg. electrode for Na-ion batteries; however, poor capacity retention has been reported and the stability of the solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) could be one of the main actors of this underperformance.  The compn. and evolution of the SEI in Na2Ti3O7 electrodes is hereby studied by means of XPS.  To overcome typical XPS limitations in the photoelectron energy assignments, the anal. of the Auger parameter is here proposed for the first time in battery materials characterization.  It was found that the electrode/electrolyte interface formed upon discharge, mostly composed by carbonates and semicarbonates (Na2CO3, NaCO3R), fluorides (NaF), chlorides (NaCl) and poly(ethylene oxide)s, is unstable upon electrochem. cycling.  Addnl., solid state NMR studies prove the reaction of the polyvinylidene difluoride (PVdF) binder with sodium.  The powerful approach used in this work, namely Auger parameter study, enables us to correctly det. the compn. of the electrode surface layer without any interference from surface charging or abs. binding energy calibration effects.  As a result, the suitability for Na-ion batteries of binders and electrolytes widely used for Li-ion batteries is questioned here.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2021.137846"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zarrabeitia, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Casas-Cabanas, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Muñoz-Márquez, M. A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Understanding the electrode – electrolyte interphase of high voltage positive electrode Na<sub>4</sub>Co<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Electrochim.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Understanding the electrode - electrolyte interphase of high voltage positive electrode Na4Co3(PO4)2P2O7 for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zarrabeitia, Maider; Casas-Cabanas, Montse; Munoz-Marquez, Miguel Angel</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">372</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) were postulated as a potential soln. for large-scale stationary applications and light electromobility.  Among pos. electrode materials for SIBs, Na4Co3(PO4)2P2O7 attracted significant attention due to its high voltage and good specific capacity even at very high current densities.  However, details of the formed electrode - electrolyte interphase (EEI) are still uncertain, being this of extreme importance considering that the high operating voltage of this electrode material is around the stability edge of most of the conventional electrolytes which, in some cases, already display side reactions &gt;3.0 V vs.  Na/Na+.  The EEI of Na4Co3(PO4)2P2O7 is analyzed in half-cell configuration using 1 M NaPF6 in EC:DEC as electrolyte.  Conventional and high energy XPS was used so as to understand the stability and the chem. compn. of the EEI.  A bilayer EEI is formed at full Na+ extd. state of charge (SOC - 4.7 V vs. Na/Na+), with semi-org.-rich species found in the subsurface region close to the electrode while more org. species are formed in the outermost surface region close to the electrolyte.  Meanwhile, after full Na+ insertion (SOC - 3.0 V vs. Na/Na+) an addnl. outermost inorg. overlayer is formed which is composed of Na carbonate and Na fluorophosphate.  Addnl., this inorg.-rich EEI is dissolving upon oxidn. / charge process - affecting the outermost ∼10 nm of the EEI.  Despite this dynamic behavior of the EEI, the Na4Co3(PO4)2P2O7 pos. electrode delivers excellent cyclability (94% capacity retention after 100 cycles at 0.2 C), proving that it can be a good candidate as pos. electrode material for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1002/celc.201600365"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nakano, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fujimura, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chihara, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Son, J.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Effect of Hexafluorophosphate and Fluoroethylene Carbonate on Electrochemical Performance and Surface Layer of Hard Carbon for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dahbi, Mouad; Nakano, Takeshi; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Fujimura, Shun; Chihara, Kuniko; Son, Jin-Young; Cui, Yi-Tao; Oji, Hiroshi; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemElectroChem</span>
